> ðŸ”— **Single vs Repeated: A Mandatory Choice**
>
> In English, "He wrote letters" is ambiguous â€” did he write letters once, or regularly? Ukrainian eliminates this ambiguity:
> - **Ð’Ñ–Ð½ Ð½Ð°Ð¿Ð¸ÑÐ°Ð² Ð»Ð¸ÑÑ‚Ð¸** = He wrote letters (once, completed)

> ðŸ”— **The Storytelling Pattern**
>
> Ukrainian narratives follow a predictable aspect pattern:
> 1. **Background** (imperfective): Set the scene â€” habits, ongoing states, descriptions
> 2. **Key events** (perfective): The plot â€” what happened, what changed
> 3. **New state** (imperfective): How things continued after the change
>
> This is similar to English "used to" â†’ "one day" â†’ "from then on," but Ukrainian encodes it directly in verb forms. When reading Ukrainian texts, notice how imperfective verbs slow the pace (description) while perfective verbs advance the plot (action).

> ðŸ”— **Marker-Aspect Matching: The Golden Rule**
>
> Think of time markers as "aspect selectors" â€” they determine which verb form you must use:
>
> | Marker Type | Examples | Required Aspect |
> |-------------|----------|-----------------|
> | Frequency | Ñ‰Ð¾Ð´Ð½Ñ, Ñ‡Ð°ÑÑ‚Ð¾, Ð·Ð°Ð²Ð¶Ð´Ð¸ | Imperfective |
> | One-time | Ð¾Ð´Ð½Ð¾Ð³Ð¾ Ñ€Ð°Ð·Ñƒ, Ñ€Ð°Ð¿Ñ‚Ð¾Ð¼, Ð½Ð°Ñ€ÐµÑˆÑ‚Ñ– | Perfective |
>
> **Mnemonic:** If the marker describes *how often*, use imperfective. If it describes *which time*, use perfective.

> ðŸ”— **Language Link**
>
> In English, habituality is often expressed through "used to" or "would" ("She used to visit every day"), while single events use simple past ("She visited yesterday"). Ukrainian grammaticalizes this distinction directly in the verb form itself, making it impossible to be ambiguous. This is why aspect mastery is essential for natural-sounding Ukrainian.
